</code></pre><p>Without <code>typedef</code>, this would use confusing <code>void**</code> and <code>void*</code> throughout.</p>
<h1>Generic Data Structure Operations</h1>
<p>Function pointers enable generic operations on <a href="./Data Structures">data structures</a>:</p>
<pre><code><span class="hljs-class"><span class="hljs-keyword">struct</span> <span class="hljs-title">node</span> {</span>
    <span class="hljs-type">int</span> key;
    <span class="hljs-type">int</span> value;
    <span class="hljs-class"><span class="hljs-keyword">struct</span> <span class="hljs-title">node</span> *<span class="hljs-title">next</span>;</span>
};

<span class="hljs-type">void</span> <span class="hljs-title function_">traverse_list</span><span class="hljs-params">(<span class="hljs-keyword">struct</span> node *<span class="hljs-built_in">list</span>, <span class="hljs-type">void</span> (*fn)(<span class="hljs-type">int</span>, <span class="hljs-type">int</span>))</span> {
    <span class="hljs-keyword">while</span> (<span class="hljs-built_in">list</span>) {
        fn(<span class="hljs-built_in">list</span>-&gt;key, <span class="hljs-built_in">list</span>-&gt;value);
        <span class="hljs-built_in">list</span> = <span class="hljs-built_in">list</span>-&gt;next;
    }
}

<span class="hljs-type">void</span> <span class="hljs-title function_">print_element</span><span class="hljs-params">(<span class="hljs-type">int</span> key, <span class="hljs-type">int</span> value)</span> {
    <span class="hljs-built_in">printf</span>(<span class="hljs-string">&quot;%d: %d\n&quot;</span>, key, value);
}

<span class="hljs-type">void</span> <span class="hljs-title function_">sum_values</span><span class="hljs-params">(<span class="hljs-type">int</span> key, <span class="hljs-type">int</span> value)</span> {
    total += value;
}

traverse_list(myList, print_element);  <span class="hljs-comment">// Print all</span>
traverse_list(myList, sum_values);     <span class="hljs-comment">// Sum all</span>
</code></pre><h1>Trade-offs</h1>
<h2>Advantages</h2>
<ul>
<li><strong>Code reuse</strong>: Write once, use with many types</li>
<li><strong>Maintainability</strong>: Changes to algorithm affect all uses</li>
<li><strong>Abstraction</strong>: Separates algorithm from data types</li>
</ul>
<h2>Disadvantages</h2>
<ul>
<li><strong>Type safety</strong>: <code>void*</code> loses compile-time type checking</li>
<li><strong>Complexity</strong>: More difficult to read and understand</li>
<li><strong>Debugging</strong>: Harder to trace through function pointer calls</li>
<li><strong>Performance</strong>: Indirect calls have slight overhead</li>
</ul>
<h1>In Other Languages</h1>
<p>Other languages provide better generic programming support:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>C++</strong>: Templates with compile-time type checking</li>
<li><strong>Java</strong>: Generics with type erasure</li>
<li><strong>Racket</strong>: Higher-order functions like <code>map</code>, <code>foldl</code>, <code>filter</code></li>
</ul>
<h1>Relationship to Polymorphism</h1>
<p>Generic programming is related to <a href="./Polymorphism">polymorphism</a> but distinct:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Generic programming</strong>: Same code for different types (parametric)</li>
<li><strong>Polymorphism</strong>: Different code for different types (subtype-based)</li>
</ul>
<p>Both use <a href="./Function Pointer">function pointers</a> in <a href="./C">C</a> but serve different purposes.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                Memory Bus
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p>The <strong>memory bus</strong> is a data and control path that connects the <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a>, <a href="./Memory">main memory</a>, and the <a href="./I-O Bus">I/O bus</a>. The memory bus operates at high speeds to match the performance requirements of the CPU and memory system.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                I-O Bus
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p>The <strong>I/O bus</strong> is a data and control path that connects the <a href="./Memory Bus">memory bus</a> to <a href="./I-O Controller">I/O controllers</a>. The I/O bus operates at a slower speed than the memory bus since <a href="./I-O Device">I/O devices</a> are typically much slower than the <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> and <a href="./Memory">main memory</a>.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                I-O Controller
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p>An <strong>I/O controller</strong> is a specialized <a href="./Processor">processor</a> that runs firmware to manage one specific type of <a href="./I-O Device">I/O device</a>. The controller connects the I/O device to the <a href="./I-O Bus">I/O bus</a>, handling the translation between the device's native protocols and the bus communication protocols.</p>
<p>Each I/O controller is typically assigned a range of <a href="./I-O Mapped Memory">I/O-mapped memory</a> addresses at boot time, allowing the <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> to communicate with it using standard <a href="./Instruction">load and store instructions</a>.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                I-O Device
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p>An <strong>I/O device</strong> is a hardware mechanism that generates or consumes data, allowing a computer system to interact with the external world. I/O devices connect to the system through <a href="./I-O Controller">I/O controllers</a>.</p>
<p>Common examples include:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Input devices</strong>: keyboard, mouse, touchscreen</li>
<li><strong>Output devices</strong>: monitor, printer, speakers</li>
<li><strong>Storage devices</strong>: hard disk, SSD, USB drive</li>
<li><strong>Network devices</strong>: Ethernet adapter, Wi-Fi card</li>
<li><strong>Imaging devices</strong>: camera, scanner</li>
</ul>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                I-O Mapped Memory
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p><strong>I/O-mapped memory</strong> refers to <a href="./Memory Address">memory addresses</a> that are beyond the end of <a href="./Memory">main memory</a> and are instead mapped to <a href="./I-O Controller">I/O controllers</a>. Each controller is assigned a specific range of addresses, typically configured at boot time.</p>
<p>When the <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> performs <a href="./Instruction">load or store instructions</a> to I/O-mapped memory addresses, these operations are translated into messages sent across the <a href="./I-O Bus">I/O bus</a> to the corresponding controller. This mechanism allows the CPU to communicate with I/O devices using the same instructions it uses for regular memory access.</p>
<p>For example, an address like <code>0x80000000</code> might be mapped to a disk controller's control registers, allowing the CPU to send commands by writing to that address.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                Programmed Input-Output
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p><strong>Programmed I/O</strong> (PIO) is a data transfer method where the <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> directly transfers data one byte at a time between itself and an <a href="./I-O Controller">I/O controller</a>. The CPU uses standard <a href="./Instruction">load and store instructions</a> to <a href="./I-O Mapped Memory">I/O-mapped memory</a> addresses to perform these transfers. PIO is always initiated by the CPU, not by the I/O controller.</p>
<h1>Limitations</h1>
<p>PIO has several significant limitations:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>CPU overhead</strong>: The CPU must actively transfer each byte, wasting cycles that could be used for computation</li>
<li><strong>Speed mismatch</strong>: <a href="./I-O Device">I/O devices</a> and controllers are much slower than the CPU, forcing the CPU to wait frequently</li>
<li><strong>Unidirectional initiation</strong>: Only the CPU can initiate transfers; controllers cannot initiate communication even when they have data available</li>
</ul>
<p>These limitations make PIO inefficient for transferring large amounts of data or handling asynchronous device events, leading to the development of <a href="./Direct Memory Access">DMA</a> and <a href="./Interrupt (Hardware)">interrupts</a>.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                Polling (Computer Science)
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p><strong>Polling</strong> is a technique where the <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> repeatedly checks an <a href="./I-O Controller">I/O controller</a> to determine if data is available or if the device is ready. The CPU uses <a href="./Programmed Input-Output">PIO</a> to read status registers from the controller in a loop until the desired condition is met.</p>
<h1>Example</h1>
<pre><code><span class="hljs-type">int</span> <span class="hljs-title function_">pollDeviceForValue</span><span class="hljs-params">()</span> {
    <span class="hljs-keyword">volatile</span> <span class="hljs-type">int</span> *ready = <span class="hljs-number">0x80000100</span>;
    <span class="hljs-keyword">volatile</span> <span class="hljs-type">int</span> *value = <span class="hljs-number">0x80000104</span>;
    <span class="hljs-keyword">while</span> (!*ready) {}  <span class="hljs-comment">// Busy-wait until ready</span>
    <span class="hljs-keyword">return</span> *value;
}
</code></pre><h1>When Polling is Acceptable</h1>
<p>Polling is acceptable when:</p>
<ul>
<li>The poll operation has low overhead</li>
<li>There is a high probability that data will be available on each poll</li>
<li>The device responds quickly</li>
</ul>
<h1>Problems with Polling</h1>
<ul>
<li><strong>CPU waste</strong>: Reading <a href="./I-O Mapped Memory">I/O-mapped memory</a> is slow, and the CPU wastes cycles waiting</li>
<li><strong>Inefficient for rare events</strong>: If data is rarely available, most polls are wasted</li>
<li><strong>Better alternatives exist</strong>: <a href="./Interrupt (Hardware)">Interrupts</a> allow the controller to signal the CPU only when needed, eliminating busy-waiting</li>
</ul>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                Direct Memory Access
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p><strong>Direct Memory Access</strong> (DMA) is a capability that allows an <a href="./I-O Controller">I/O controller</a> to directly transfer data to and from <a href="./Memory">main memory</a> without <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> involvement. DMA enables the CPU and I/O controllers to work in parallel, with controllers autonomously moving data while the CPU performs other tasks.</p>
<h1>Operation</h1>
<p>The CPU configures DMA transfers using <a href="./Programmed Input-Output">PIO</a> to send the controller:</p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="./Memory Address">memory address</a> for the transfer</li>
<li>The number of bytes to transfer</li>
<li>The direction (read from or write to memory)</li>
</ul>
<p>Once configured, the controller performs the transfer independently. When complete, the controller typically signals the CPU using an <a href="./Interrupt (Hardware)">interrupt</a>.</p>
<h1>Advantages</h1>
<ul>
<li><strong>Parallelism</strong>: The CPU can continue executing instructions while the controller transfers data</li>
<li><strong>Efficiency</strong>: Large data transfers don't consume CPU cycles</li>
<li><strong>Performance</strong>: The system can overlap computation with I/O operations</li>
</ul>
<p>DMA is essential for high-performance I/O operations such as disk reads/writes and network packet transfers.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                Interrupt (Hardware)
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p>A hardware <strong>interrupt</strong> is a signal from an <a href="./I-O Controller">I/O controller</a> to the <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> that requests immediate attention. Interrupts allow controllers to initiate communication with the CPU, signaling events such as I/O completion, error conditions, or incoming data.</p>
<h1>Hardware Implementation</h1>
<p>The CPU includes special registers for interrupt handling:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>isDeviceInterrupting</strong>: Set by the I/O controller when it needs attention</li>
<li><strong>interruptControllerID</strong>: Identifies which controller is interrupting</li>
<li><strong>interruptVectorBase</strong>: Points to the interrupt vector table (initialized at boot)</li>
</ul>
<p>The CPU checks <code>isDeviceInterrupting</code> on every clock cycle—a fast hardware poll. When an interrupt is detected, the CPU:</p>
<ol>
<li>Saves its current state on the <a href="./Stack">stack</a></li>
<li>Jumps to the appropriate <strong>Interrupt Service Routine</strong> (ISR) using the interrupt vector table</li>
<li>Executes the ISR</li>
<li>Returns to the interrupted program using a special RTI (Return from Interrupt) instruction</li>
</ol>
<h1>Interrupt Vector Table</h1>
<p>The <strong>interrupt vector table</strong> is an array of <a href="./Function Pointer">function pointers</a>, indexed by controller ID, that points to each controller's ISR. This table is initialized by the <a href="./Operating System">operating system</a> at boot time.</p>
<h1>Advantages</h1>
<ul>
<li><strong>Efficiency</strong>: Eliminates <a href="./Polling (Computer Science)">polling</a> overhead by allowing controllers to signal only when needed</li>
<li><strong>Asynchrony</strong>: Controllers can signal unexpected events (e.g., mouse clicks, network packets)</li>
<li><strong>Parallelism</strong>: The CPU doesn't waste cycles waiting for I/O; it can perform useful work until interrupted</li>
</ul>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                Asynchronous Operation
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p>An <strong>asynchronous operation</strong> is one that is initiated but does not complete immediately, allowing the program to continue executing while the operation proceeds in the background. The operation's completion is signaled later through some notification mechanism.</p>
<h1>Comparison to Synchronous Operations</h1>
<p>In a <strong>synchronous operation</strong>, the program waits (blocks) until the operation completes:</p>
<pre><code><span class="hljs-comment">// Synchronous - program waits here</span>
<span class="hljs-type">char</span> data = readDiskBlock(blockNum);
processData(data);  <span class="hljs-comment">// Runs after read completes</span>
</code></pre><p>In an <strong>asynchronous operation</strong>, the program continues immediately:</p>
<pre><code><span class="hljs-comment">// Asynchronous - program continues immediately</span>
readDiskBlock(blockNum, processDataCallback);
doOtherWork();  <span class="hljs-comment">// Runs while disk read is in progress</span>
<span class="hljs-comment">// processDataCallback runs later when read completes</span>
</code></pre><h1>Challenges</h1>
<p>Asynchronous operations break the natural sequential flow of programs:</p>
<ol>
<li><strong>Order of execution</strong>: Code written sequentially may execute in a different order</li>
<li><strong>Data dependencies</strong>: Values computed asynchronously aren't immediately available</li>
<li><strong>Control flow</strong>: The program must be restructured around callbacks or event handlers</li>
</ol>
<h1>Example Problem</h1>
<pre><code><span class="hljs-comment">// This code appears sequential but won&#x27;t work!</span>
<span class="hljs-type">char</span> <span class="hljs-title function_">checksumDiskData</span><span class="hljs-params">(<span class="hljs-type">int</span> blockNum, <span class="hljs-type">int</span> numBytes)</span> {
    <span class="hljs-type">char</span> buf[numBytes];
    <span class="hljs-type">char</span> xsum = <span class="hljs-number">0</span>;
    requestDiskRead(buf, blockNum, numBytes);  <span class="hljs-comment">// Asynchronous!</span>
    <span class="hljs-keyword">for</span> (<span class="hljs-type">int</span> i = <span class="hljs-number">0</span>; i &lt; numBytes; i++)
        xsum ^= buf[i];  <span class="hljs-comment">// buf not yet filled!</span>
    <span class="hljs-keyword">return</span> xsum;
}
</code></pre><p>The disk read is asynchronous, so the for loop executes before the data arrives. The code must be restructured to use <a href="./Event-Driven Programming">event handlers</a> or other asynchronous patterns.</p>
<p>Asynchronous operations are necessary for performance when dealing with slow <a href="./I-O Device">I/O devices</a>, but they make programs significantly harder to write and debug. Modern systems use <a href="./Interrupt (Hardware)">interrupts</a> and <a href="./Direct Memory Access">DMA</a> to enable asynchronous I/O.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                Event-Driven Programming
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p><strong>Event-driven programming</strong> is a programming paradigm where the flow of the program is determined by events—occurrences such as I/O completions, user interactions, or timer expirations. Code is structured around event handlers (callbacks) that execute asynchronously when their associated events occur.</p>
<h1>Key Concepts</h1>
<ul>
<li><strong>Event</strong>: An occurrence that triggers code execution (e.g., disk read completion, mouse click)</li>
<li><strong>Event Handler</strong>: A <a href="./Function Pointer">function</a> that executes when a specific event occurs</li>
<li><strong>Event Registration</strong>: Associating a handler with an event</li>
<li><strong>Event Firing</strong>: Triggering the execution of registered handlers</li>
</ul>
<h1>Example</h1>
<pre><code><span class="hljs-type">void</span> <span class="hljs-title function_">computeChecksum</span><span class="hljs-params">(<span class="hljs-type">char</span> *buf, <span class="hljs-type">int</span> blockNum, <span class="hljs-type">int</span> numBytes)</span> {
    <span class="hljs-type">char</span> xsum = <span class="hljs-number">0</span>;
    <span class="hljs-keyword">for</span> (<span class="hljs-type">int</span> i = <span class="hljs-number">0</span>; i &lt; numBytes; i++)
        xsum ^= buf[i];
    <span class="hljs-built_in">printf</span>(<span class="hljs-string">&quot;Checksum: %d\n&quot;</span>, xsum);
    <span class="hljs-built_in">free</span>(buf);
}

<span class="hljs-type">void</span> <span class="hljs-title function_">checksumDiskData</span><span class="hljs-params">(<span class="hljs-type">int</span> blockNum, <span class="hljs-type">int</span> numBytes)</span> {
    <span class="hljs-type">char</span> *buf = <span class="hljs-built_in">malloc</span>(numBytes);
    <span class="hljs-comment">// Register handler and initiate read</span>
    diskRead(buf, blockNum, numBytes, computeChecksum);
    <span class="hljs-comment">// Function returns immediately; handler runs later</span>
}
</code></pre><h1>Challenges</h1>
<p>Event-driven code is inherently more difficult than sequential code:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Callback hell</strong>: Deeply nested callbacks make code hard to read and maintain</li>
<li><strong>Control flow</strong>: The execution order is non-obvious from reading the code</li>
<li><strong>Debugging</strong>: Stack traces are fragmented across multiple callback invocations</li>
<li><strong>State management</strong>: Sharing data between handlers requires careful management</li>
</ul>
<h1>Why Use Event-Driven Programming</h1>
<p>Despite the difficulties, event-driven programming is necessary when dealing with <a href="./Asynchronous Operation">asynchronous operations</a> like <a href="./I-O Device">I/O</a>. The <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> can execute millions of instructions while waiting for a single disk read; event-driven code allows useful work during these waits.</p>
<p>Event-driven programming is common in:</p>
<ul>
<li>GUI applications (handling user input events)</li>
<li>Web programming (JavaScript, Node.js)</li>
<li><a href="./Operating System">Operating systems</a> (handling <a href="./Interrupt (Hardware)">interrupts</a>)</li>
<li>Network programming (handling incoming connections and data)</li>
</ul>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                Thread
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p>A <strong>thread</strong> is an abstraction that allows a program to execute multiple independent streams of <a href="./Control Flow">control</a> concurrently on a <a href="./Processor">single physical processor</a>. Threads can be thought of as lightweight <a href="./Program">processes</a> that share the same <a href="./Memory Address">address</a> space and resources.</p>
<p>Threads enable programs to handle <a href="./Asynchronous Operation">asynchronous operations</a>, such as input and output, without blocking the entire <a href="./Program">program</a>. Instead of waiting for an operation to complete, a thread can be paused and resumed, allowing other threads to run in the meantime.</p>
<h1>States</h1>
<p>A thread can be in one of several states:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Nascent</strong>: Newly created but not yet runnable</li>
<li><strong>Runnable</strong>: Ready to execute but waiting for <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> time</li>
<li><strong>Running</strong>: Currently executing on the <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a></li>
<li><strong>Blocked</strong>: Waiting for an event (e.g., I/O completion)</li>
<li><strong>Dead</strong>: Finished execution</li>
</ul>
<p>Transitions between states are managed by the operating system or thread library.</p>
<h1>Operations</h1>
<h2>Creation</h2>
<p>Threads are created by a <a href="./Procedure">function</a> which typically takes a <a href="./Procedure">procedure</a> and arguments. This &quot;forks&quot; the <a href="./Control Flow">control flow</a>, starting a new thread that runs concurrently.</p>
<h2>Blocking and Unblocking</h2>
<ul>
<li><strong>Block</strong>: Save the thread's state and stops it until unblocked.</li>
<li><strong>Unblock</strong>: Unblock moves a blocked thread back to runnable.</li>
</ul>
<h2>Joining</h2>
<p>Joining a thread blocks the current thread until the joined thread completes, optionally retrieving its return value. This provides synchronous behavior.</p>
<h2>Yielding</h2>
<p>Yielding temporarily pauses the current thread, allowing others to run. Notably the thread remains runnable.</p>
<h1>Switching</h1>
<p>Thread switching occurs at the instruction level:</p>
<ol>
<li>Save current thread's registers to its stack</li>
<li>Save stack pointer to thread control block</li>
<li>Load new thread's stack pointer from its thread control block</li>
<li>Restore registers from the new stack</li>
</ol>
<p>The <a href="./Program Counter">program counter</a> is handled implicitly via the stack, as the return address is saved/restored.</p>
<h1>Scheduling Policies</h1>
<p>Scheduling policies determine which runnable thread is selected to run next on the <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> when multiple threads are ready. They balance competing goals such as fairness (equal <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> time), responsiveness (quick reaction to events), priority (important tasks first), and efficiency (maximizing throughput). Policies can be preemptive (interrupting running threads) or non-preemptive (threads run until they yield or block).</p>
<h2>Round-robin</h2>
<p>In round-robin scheduling, threads take turns running for a fixed time slice (typically 10-100ms). When a thread's time expires, it is preempted and placed back in the runnable queue, allowing the next thread to run. This ensures fair sharing of <a href="./Central Processing Unit">CPU</a> time among threads of equal priority, preventing any single thread from monopolizing the processor.</p>
<p>Round robin may not prioritize urgent tasks and can introduce overhead from frequent context switches.</p>
<h2>Priority-based</h2>
<p>Priority-based scheduling assigns each thread a priority level, with higher-priority threads running before lower ones. Within the same priority, round-robin is often used. This is useful for systems where certain tasks (e.g., user interface updates or real-time processing) must respond quickly.</p>
<p>Priorities can be static (fixed) or dynamic (adjusted based on behavior). A drawback is starvation, where low-priority threads may never run if high-priority ones dominate.</p>
<h2>Preemption</h2>
<p>Preemption allows the scheduler to interrupt a running thread to switch to another, improving responsiveness. It occurs in two main ways: priority preemption (when a higher-priority thread becomes runnable) and time-based preemption (at the end of a time slice). This is implemented using timer interrupts that periodically check if a switch is needed.</p>
<p>Preemptive scheduling is essential for interactive and real-time systems but adds complexity, as threads must handle being interrupted at any point.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                Lock
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p>A lock is a primitive for controlling synchronization between concurrent <a href="./Program">programs</a>. Lock's prevent state from being accessed by multiple <a href="./Thread">threads</a> at the same time. A <a href="./Thread">thread</a> can perform two operations on a lock, attempting to acquire it or release it. When attempting to acquire a lock if it's already &quot;held&quot; by another <a href="./Thread">thread</a> the acquiring <a href="./Thread">thread</a> will enter a <a href="./Thread#States">waiting state</a> until the lock is released by the holding <a href="./Thread">thread</a>.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
<p>
            <div class="embed">
              <div class="embed-page">
                Asynchronous Condition Variable
              </div>
              <div class="embed-content">
                <p><mjx-container class="MathJax" jax="CHTML" style="font-size: 121%;" display="true"><mjx-math display="true" style="margin-left: 0; margin-right: 0;" class=" MJX-TEX"></mjx-math></mjx-container></p>
<p>An asynchronous condition variable is a primitive for controlling synchronization between concurrent <a href="./Program">programs</a>, they pause the execution of certain <a href="./Thread">threads</a> until it receives a signal to continue their execution.</p>
<p>A <a href="./Thread">thread</a> can perform two operations on an asynchronous condition variable, it can either wait for the condition variable to signal, or broadcast which releases all waiting threads.</p>

              </div>
            </div></p>
